You'll learn about \\"approach\\" notes, which are central in melodic and jazz bass and extends to most current music styles. 0375714839|5.0|I realize it's a cliche, but the book is always better than the animated feature (which was excellent). Still, with The Complete Persepolis, I discovered so many depths that I missed in the film. One thing that was so cool was that even in repressive societies, such as Satrapi's revolution-era Iran, she was an Iron Maiden fan like me. There's always something hopeful about the youth in these cultures, that they always find a way to flout the rules. It's an eye-opening glimpse into a world few of us in the West truly know much about, save for what we're shown on television news--which is always suspect.<br /><br />In spite of the fact that I own an e-reader, this was a book that just had to be savored physically, and I enjoyed every beautifully illustrated page.<br /><br />Marjane's struggles as a young woman are universal. All young women worry about fitting in, about doing what's \\"expected\\", about whether they're going to find the right \\"one\\". We often wander in this search for self and find ourselves lost. It happens in Los Angeles Like most human beings we're often our own worst critics. I cheered at Marjane's bravery for speaking out against over-thr-top strictures concerning the veil and other things. It was both hilarious and humbling, when one considers that she could have been killed for being so outspoken. In fact, many of her family and friends had been jailed/killed for even smaller acts of \\"defiance\\".<br /><br />An illustrated novel such as this with these universal themes set against the backdrop of war, repression and state-sanctioned murder once again makes me wonder why there are authors who have no problems crafting vampires and werewolves, but can't seem to grasp the universalism of human beings of varying hues.<br /><br />My favorite character by far is Marjane's grandmother. Much like grandmothers of every stripe, shade and size, she is a fountain of great wisdom, strength and comfort. So glad that I finally got around to reading this. Go Iron Maiden!iden!

0525423281|4.0|I read Lola and the Boy Next Door by Stephanie Perkins as an audiobook. I started Perkins' other book, Anna and the French Kiss, before I started Lola, but was having a hard time getting into it. Once I started reading/listening to Lola, I didn't stop for two days. The reason is simple: Lola is more likeable than Anna.<br /><br />Granted, there are plenty of things that make Lola a flawed character. She lies frequently in order to keep the peace -- with her dads, with her friends, her boyfriend, etc. On the one hand, it's admirable that she cares enough about these people to protect their feelings, but Lola tells some selfish lies as well. Regardless of her flaws, Lola is interesting. I had a hard time caring about Anna because she's very girl-next-door, almost blandly so, but Lola (ironically) is anything but.<br /><br />Lola is a budding costume designer, and since she was a little girl she has lived every day of her life in costume. She dresses up as characters, themes, inanimate objects -- all tastefully, of course. She's over-the-top, with wigs, makeup, and a self-made wardrobe comprised of recycled and repurposed garments from thrift stores. She's one of those fascinating creative types, and hearing about her wardrobe projects/inspiration brought new levels of depth and fun to the narrative. She would seem superficial if her plans for the future didn't revolve around designing costumes for theatre -- she's practicing for adulthood.<br /><br />Joining Lola as she navigates first love are her parents, Nathan and Andy, her birth mother/aunt Nora, best friend and sleuth Lucy, boyfriend/rocker Max, and boy-next-door Cricket Bell. Each of them is well developed and distinct, teaching Lola that love isn't always easy, the ideal lover might not be the right lover, and that breaking up is hard to do.<br /><br />As an audiobook narrator, Shannon McManus does a good job of bringing the voice of teenaged Lola to life. I had a giggle the first time the word panniers came up, though, with flat American pronunciation. Can't win `em all, I guess.<br /><br />Lola and the Boy Next Door is a fun, light YA contemporary. It's a well-paced, balanced story with endearing characters and a memorable protagonist. Give it a try on your next rainy day at home. I guarantee Lola will bring a little sunshine into you life.

0307352145|5.0|\\"Quiet\\" by Susan Cain is brilliantly done in explaining the introverted self.  She starts the book by giving a brief history lesson on the rise of \\"the extroverted ideal\\".  At the turn of the 19th century the \\"rise of the Culture of Personality\\" took hold among the American population.  Infatuation of movie stars, and advertising only hastened the change from the \\"culture of character\\" to that of personality.<br /><br />The author delves into the different aspects of life where the extroverted ideal reigns.  She shows how the ideal touches on the entire school life from pre-school through college.  Also, how the ideal is prevalent in the work place.  After showing examples of the ideal and how it touches most every part of our life, she goes into the biological studies on the introverted mind.<br /><br />Author Susan Cain stays away from the in depth medical terms, which makes this portion an easy read.  She includes studies that are not only short term in nature, i.e. brain scans, but also long term studies that started  with infants and concluded when they were college students.  She then devotes a chapter on how the introverted mind can go beyond itself and stretch its boundaries.<br /><br />The final part of the book the author offers suggestions on how to love and how to work.  She offers some advice on both personal relationships and work relationships.  The biggest point she drove home was for an introvert to find their quiet time.<br /><br />This book is wonderfully put together, Susan Cain touches on so many different aspects of the introverted life/mind while not getting too technical in her descriptions.  This book is excellent for both the extroverts as well as introverts we all meet.  I would highly recommend this book to anyone in a leadership position.<br /><br />Disclaimer:  I received this book for free from WaterBrook Multnomah Publishing Group for this review.

1469984202|5.0|If you are reading this, then the title of my review has done it's job & I am glad for it.  Having cut my teeth on the works of Asimov, Clarke, Niven, Bradbury, Heinlein, etc, it has been a bit of a challenge to find more modern authors who have the ability to tell a story rich in character development, gripping story, imaginative, yet believable.<br /><br />So, since book reviews are meant to guide the next possible reader, let me cut to the chase first, and then I will go to work on the review of the books:<br /><br />******* BUY THIS SERIES NOW! *******<br /><br />Do not waste your time trying the sample, just go ahead and buy all of the works in the WOOL collection (six, as of this writing) right now.<br /><br />If you do not buy them all, and you find yourself without an internet connection and you just finished reading one of the books and you know there are others available you will be extremely annoyed, distracted, pi$$ed off, etc till you get the next one & start reading it.<br /><br />Having read hundreds of books, many in the SciFi genre, but also many biographies, and historical works (european & british history especially) I was absolutely blown away by the Wool books.  To be completely honest, I almost put the book down maybe 30 pages into it. I was on vacation on an amazing little island off the coast of North Carolina, no cars, no distractions except miles of beaches & ocean waves when I searched on my Kindle for some Science Fiction to read over the 15 days I was away.  Since I often read SciFi books that are based well in the future, take place off-earth, etc, I was not really prepared for the way Wool opened. It was dark, depressing, and not what I thought I wanted on my vacation with the sun shining and the seagulls singing and the waves crashing on the sand. But I stuck with it, and that is why I am here today, writing this.<br /><br />First of all lets backup for a second and talk about the name of the book. WOOL. You wear it (sometimes).  Butit has numerous other uses, including, apparently, as the name of a book.  I didn't know what to even make of it.  I mean look at most of the titles under SciFi, & you get the space war sagas, the alien invasions, the boy-meets-space travel stories, etc....  But WOOL?? I couldnt fathom it would be a book that would interest me for very long, and so I again reveal how mistaken I can be.  Important Lesson learned? : Dont judge a book by it's cover, nor by it's NAME.<br /><br />One of the many great yet very subtle parts of this work is when you actually connect the name WOOL with the line in the story that reveals the connection.  It's not too far into book 1 and you know what, you might not even catch it, and it might not mean much to you- but it did, for some reason, to me.<br /><br />It brought home to me that Hugh Howey had put me in a place that seemed at first to be so incomprehensible, but later came to be seen as a very possible, plausible place not so far removed as to be unimaginable, yet still so staggering in it's implications.<br /><br />If WOOL had only been the short, single work of book 1, I would have been upset that it wasnt taken further, or approached it's potential.  In fact, I didn't know there were more books at first and I really was upset. I thought \\"WOW- SO MUCH POTENTIAL, this was an amazing story, but look where it ended!?\\"  And then I checked back on Amazon and saw the remaining books and grabbed them all (hence my earlier suggestion to do the same).<br /><br />Without spoiling the story for those of you who have not yet started, I do want to say that this is one of the most fascinating, smart, stop-for-a-second-and-say- `Oh my god!' reads I have ever picked up.<br /><br />I absolutely loved the way I first tried to figure out where this was taking place.  Was it on earth?  Somewhere else?  When?  How did it come to be...?  Did any of that matter...?  As I read on, and parts of those questions were  answered I was very very pleased with the depth of the story, and so glad that there wasn't a flaky, thin, all-is-revealed at once to put it all together for me.  It came together carefully, methodically, and at just the right pace.<br />The fact that the characters in WOOL are just people, with no super abilities, paranormal talents, etc, makes them all the more important to the way you see them move through the story.<br /><br />While all of the books were gripping to me, I particularly enjoyed the flashbacks to see how it all came about.  Without spoiling this particular part, I will just say that it was extremely well done and has some fantastic but subtle connections between the characters and story that could easily be missed.  A great example of this: A sister of a congressman only gets mentioned briefly and almost as an aside in passing during a brief conversation, yet later you realize the implications of the circumstances and again have another \\"WOW\\" moment.<br /><br />Yes,  I realize that was pretty vague, but trust me on it.  If you miss it, my apologies, if you get it, no other comment is required..;)<br /><br />Well, this is by far the longest review I have ever written and I still feel like it is not even close to being as compelling and passionate as I had envisioned it when I decided it had to be done.  But in closing just a couple of things to say:  No doubt, this is a book I will read many times over, and as another reviewer summed it up so well, I only wish I could read it again for the first time.<br /><br />Will it become a movie?  I don't know, but I hope so, but ONLY if it can be done well (an example of what it should NOT be like: Steven King 'The Stand' book vs. movie.  The movie was an insult to the book in my opinion).  The Scott brothers certainly have the ability to do it right.  I actually care less about the movie possibilities than the fact that the deal may help Mr. Howey devote more time to writing!  We will see....  Regardless of the outcome of the motion picture issue,I hope that Mr. Howey keeps this series going for a long time.  I do understand that talented authors, artists, actors, musicians, etc need to move on and create new works and new characters and new stories, but the selfish part of me hopes this story will keep intriguing us and challenging us for years to come in new installments and characters and situations.  The foundation exists in as solid a form as possible.<br /><br />In my opinion this is already a classic work of fiction, here to stay, and Mr. Howey should feel extremely pleased to be in this stratosphere of talent that comes along so rarely.  These books will be talked about in classrooms, in living rooms, family rooms, bookstores, etc for a long time to come and I really hope you will enjoy them as much as I have.

B0045HCJKS|3.0|Written and directed by Michael Crichton, Westworld revolves around the concept of a distant-future amusement resort. Spending their time in one of three fully-functional, authentic sets, guests are free to live out their life-long fantasies as a wild west cowboy, medieval knight or indulgent Roman elite while a convincing, lifelike cyborg supporting cast absorbs punishment and boosts egos. The big selling points are that anything goes and nobody gets hurt, but when a park-wide malfunction grants free will to the robots, those guarantees are immediately called into question. It's a fun premise, on par with some of the era's better sci-fi concepts, but spends too much time dilly-dallying and vainly soaking up the atmosphere when it should be advancing the plot. That gives the impression that, like so many Philip Dick properties, it doesn't really know what to do with itself after the ground rules are established. Crichton's work in the director's chair leaves a lot to be desired, but he doesn't get much help from the film's hammy, made-for-TV quality special effects. Most of that can be attributed to the age of the picture, but it's tough to take the drama seriously when folks bleed neon red. Yul Brynner turns in some quality work as the ruthless, stone-faced lead cyborg, but the rest of the cast is droll, vanilla and forgettable. Too much frosting, not enough cake.

0307592731|5.0|I must have considered reading Wild about a dozen times over the past couple of months - every time I browsed the Editor's Picks on my Kindle.  It kept popping up.  I would think, \\"yeah, well I'm interested in getting started with backpacking. Maybe this is the book to inspire me,\\" and then I'd read the description of the book and respond to myself with \\"well, she was a few years younger than me when she did this hike and it seems like it's going to focus more on sentimentality and introspection than the actual hike itself.\\"  I've read more than enough \\"person experiencing tough situations in life and so takes a spiritual journey to find herself\\" memoirs. I also am a bit unnecessarily ageist when it comes to anything autobiographical and didn't think she'd have the self-awareness at 26 that I'd relate to at my somewhat older age. When I read that she'd made the hike in the 90's and was now in her 40s, I changed my mind a bit - ridiculous reason to finally give it a chance, I know.<br /><br />Cheryl Strayed's memoir is different. She doesn't just venture ever-so-gently into self-reflective territory; she's unflinchingly honest, her emotions painfully raw. We're not just told about her sadness when her mother dies; we bear witness to her suffering.  She doesn't simply imply she's made questionable decisions with men, but gives us every bit of evidence to prove it.  While laying bare her weaknesses, I could easily see her strength of character.<br /><br />Inspiration is an over-used word to describe memoirs, no doubt, but how else can I describe my own feelings from almost the very beginning of this book. Cheryl sets out to do something for no specific purpose other than it occurred to her to do it. At that point in her life, she felt so lost that she needed to do something huge to save herself. The Pacific Crest Trail was huge.  I know that feeling, and it's just so easy to imagine being in Cheryl's place, feeling desperate, stubbornly following through on a decisiondespite having doubts just because you know, for some unknown reason, it's important.<br /><br />As an avid memoir reader, I generally hope to get, at the least, new insight into a world I know little about.  At the best, I hope to feel what the author felt, see what she saw and be as much there sense-wise as I can be. Wild is lovely that way.  Cheryl's a wonderful, vivid writer.  She doesn't overwhelm us with inane detail but the ones she chooses to give out are there for a specific pupose. When she approaches a water tank on a brutally hot day only to see a 'no water' sign, I can almost cry from the disappointment. When she sits alone in her tent, her only protection against the harshness of nature, I can imagine the physical relief and pain of a body coming to rest after pushing it to its limits.  I can feel the heaviness of a giant backpack on my own shoulders as she walks, the pain of too-tight boots, the ever-present danger of men potentially preying on single female hikers and the surreal embarrassment of being forced to wear duct-tape sandals. Most of all, I can sense the dark and sad thoughts swirling and whirling around and around as she immerses herself in solitude.<br /><br />I've read other adventure memoirs that have disappointed for lack of introspection; they function more as a way of laying down the facts. I'm currently reading AWOL on the Appalachian Trail after seeing it recommended by several people who enjoyed the backpacking adventures of Wild, but after experiencing Cheryl's emotional and physical upheaval, it reads as more of a guidebook, monotone in voice, lacking any form of despair, intensity and hilarity, all of which Cheryl gives us in abundance.<br /><br />Some other reviewers have criticized Cheryl for her sexual choices and heroin use, but it's important to note that she's not defending her behaviors, just simply being truthful about them. While's she's not proud of some of her decisions, she doesn't hide in shame either. Such openness should be lauded rather than judged. She reveals herself as a person just as full of flaws, quirks, pain and humor as we know ourselves to be, and that's what makes the journey of the author such a rich experience for the reader.

It focuses on dialog and moving the plot along, at the expense of description and introspection. The characters are often difficult to distinguish (you have to remember their names) and scenes take place in featureless voids.<br /><br />Even so, it was fun. I wish I could give it another half of a star.

Not particularly a bad thing but not exactly groundbreaking either.  From here on out the group goes on autopilot and we are treated to familiar sounds and structures.  \\"Depraved Mind Murder\\" could have been on \\"Archetype.\\"  Seems Dino took the initiative to rip off Christian for ripping him off on that particular album.  \\"Virus of Faith,\\" \\"Difference Engine,\\" and \\"Disassemble\\" sound like they could be on \\"Obsolete\\" or \\"Demanufacture.\\"  Once again not really a bad thing but for a band that lays its foundation on being innovative it seems they are using old tricks.  The album closers \\"Religion is Flawed...\\" is a short piano piece and \\"Human Augmentation\\" is an ambient electronic piece.  This seems like a lazy approach to close an album because they already did this on \\"Remanufacture\\" and \\"Archetype.\\"  Even lazier considering they can barely do their own electronics when they have Rhys Fulber at the helm.  He is vital though because he gives Fear Factory their sound.  Without him they lose a lot of atmosphere and depth.  Listen to \\"Transgression\\" and \\"Soul of a New Machine\\" for proof. The story itself is okay when listening to the album but kind of boring on its own and is derivative of Blade Runner.  So overall the album is decent but it does come across as sounding a bit too familiar at times.  It seems like they started on an album that showed promise and innovation but when they decided to go with the concept they slipped back into comfortable territory.  3 1/2 Stars.

B004LWZWGA|5.0|Take heed: there be spoilers ahead<br /><br />\\"If you make yourself more than just a man, if you devote yourself to an ideal... you become something else entirely. A legend, Mr. Wayne, a legend!\\" Here we are, seven years since Christopher Nolan saved the Batman legacy from the pit of bat crap that it fell into during the `90s. Batman Begins was, as the name implies, a fresh beginning, a reimagining of something that held great potential in a post 9/11culture. So, it is no surprise that the words of Ra's al Ghul should bring us full circle in The Dark Knight Rises. These words, \\"...an ideal...a legend...\\" expose the shadow of Liam Neeson's too-benevolent cultist throughout Nolan's trilogy. They unmask the difference between men and their legacies; they contrast the polarizing sacrifice Ra's al Ghul made with the Bat's sacrifices; and they reveal a story not just about superheroes and absurd villains but symbols, ideals, icons and the things which make them.<br /><br />The Dark Knight Rises begins with two symbols bred into Gotham's collective unconscious eight years earlier with the death of the white knight, Harvey Dent, and the betrayal of the dark knight, Batman. The Batman, billionaire Bruce Wayne's alter-ego, has gone into retirement. Meanwhile, Gotham is getting along well under the guidance of commissioner Jim Gordon (played once more by the always-talented Gary Oldman) and vigorous, noble cops like John Blake (Joseph Gordon-Levitt). That is until a slinky Selena Kyle sets into motion a chain of events that lead to the arrival of one of Batman's most notorious villains, Bane (Tom Hardy).<br /><br />The plot that unfolds involves monstrous action scenes, looming cityscapes, and a music score that, while not at all subtle, does exemplify Nolan's current embrace of epic, booming sounds. That said, if you come to this movie looking for constant emotional grip and release, you will certainly find it, not to mention a cast that becomes itself in terms of character and character development. You will also find socio-political satire that comments on both wings of American politics. And surprisingly, there is a comic lightness (in act 1) that was missing in The Dark Knight. But, above all of this, what really makes this film worth watching is Nolan's insistence on converging the physical world of Gotham, the mental planes of Bruce Wayne's mind, and the metaphysical shroud that makes objects like the Batsuit, Batcave, and Pit so enthralling for audiences. So how does he do this? That is where the making of symbols comes in.<br /><br />Throughout this trilogy, Nolan has taken a man and conveyed physicality, mentality, and metaphysicality all through his desire to create a society capable of knowing good. Of course, this is paradoxical--how does a man become more than a man and less than a man at the same time--and it is what drives so many to wonder if the Bat will die or live on at the end of this movie. Because ultimately, we come to realize that a man cannot live as a person and become a symbol at the same time. While he lives, he is judged by the actions he makes and will make. But, when he dies, like Harvey Dent and Ra's al Ghul, he is seen by what he has done. In truth, he becomes a legend.nd character development. How Ender's brother and sister managed to become widely recognized, published writers and thinkers at such young ages, anonymously on the nets. I think there's going to come a time when all people have their own online presence, and also freely create fictitious, anonymous personages for themselves, and I think this could be an incredibly healthy thing to be able to do, especially for the young.<br /><br />Why? Because people want to be able to say what is truly on their mind, experiment with their ability to express themselves, and, if they happen to take an experiment a bit too far, have the luxury of simply deleting that character and starting fresh. At the time Card wrote Ender's Game it seemed such ability to post anonymously was only going to become a richer, better experience. Sadly, the opposite is becoming true. Our online footprints are easier to trace than anything else we do. I hope that changes.<br /><br />But it is a fascinating aspect of Ender's Game that the title character's siblings, bred to be impossibly talented, find this mechanism to gain influence over the world.<br /><br />Another element to the story is the actual control interface Ender uses during certain battles, how it is three dimensional in a way that makes his 3d battle school weightless training relevant. I won't try to give away more than that, other than to say that not only was Card many years ahead of his time, Ender's Game holds up because it is simply a beautiful story. It seems to me that fans can tell when an author has fallen in love with his own characters.

B004LWZWGA|5.0|.<br />AS ALWAYS, NO SPOILERS HERE:<br /><br />How difficult must it have been for Christopher Nolan to have put together this movie?  With such lofty hopes (and $$) placed upon his shoulders, such fan expectations?  And then, to have had the terrible tragedy attached to its premiere...<br /><br />\\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" really had a mountain to climb in order to even come close to pleasing the general public.<br /><br />And, the verdict?<br /><br />Here it is: \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is absolutely amazing.  I loved this movie, and consider it a remarkable achievement.  Sure...there were a few faults with it (and I'll address them in a few paragraphs) but all in all Nolan has provided us with not only a great adventure and a dramatically compelling storyline, but also a fitting ending to Nolan's trilogy.  It is a graphic novel with real gravitas, a fully considered finale to a sweeping epic.<br /><br />And DON'T WORRY, I will NOT include any spoilers as I discuss it.<br /><br />First of all: you should know what kind of movie you're going to see.  For those about to leave home to plop themselves down in a (hopefully) comfortable movie theater, \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is not \\"The Avengers\\"...for good reason.  While the latter film is all action, action, action from the get-go, \\"TDKR\\" is much more drama-oriented.<br /><br />I've heard some complaints from people who said it was too slow, or too long, and this equated to boredom.  However, I think they simply didn't realize that Christopher Nolan is a serious director, and wanted to tell a \\"real\\" and serious story, not just create a typical pop comic strip in film format.  This is not to knock \\"The Avengers\\"--I think it's one terrific movie, one of the best I've seen in recent years.  Of its type.<br /><br />The beauty of \\"TDKR\\" and its predecessors is that Nolan and his brother Jonathan have created a three-story arc that sets a tale into motion, full of backstory and motivation, progresses that narrative through an exciting and sweeping character-driven tale that questions not only the ethics and morality of a single, powerful law officer, but then examines what outcome such an iconic man might expect.<br /><br />IF HE WERE REAL, in other words, what toll would crime-fighting take upon such a figurehead, especially given the motivations of those who chose to battle him, and how might a man of Batman's stature hope to conclude such an occupation\\"?  He couldn't expect to go on forever--so how might his story be resolved?<br /><br />In other words, this is not your grand-daddy's Batman who just went on forever, dispatching foes by the end of every comic book.  Nor is this even the movie begun under Tim Burton's initially competent hand, unfortunately fizzling out and all but sent to the death chamber under the inept Joel Schumacher (\\"Batman Beyond\\").<br /><br />No, \\"TDKR\\" is a real and plausible third and final chapter for this particular Batman legend --and please note that I am leaving the ending completely open to all possibilities.  I am only saying that Nolan is done; he is not coming back and he has fashioned his film in this manner.<br /><br />Okay: \\"TDKR\\" is not a perfect movie, as there were some rough edges in the story-telling.  For one thing, the time scale seemed off as the story progressed from Bane's takeover of Gotham City and neared its end...you weren't prepped as to when weeks between scenes switched into hours and then into minutes.  And certain developments weren't clear, either: where was Batman placed after his first entanglement with Bane?  And in this post-revolution city, which police officers were imprisoned and which weren't--and how did those who weren't stay out of the jailors' reach?<br /><br />Some of these questions will probably be resolved for me upon further viewings...and I do plan to see this film over and over again.  As with other really good movies, one can expect to see deeper and deeper levels every time they watch.  Christopher Nolan isone of those directors, and he populates his movies with all sorts of important dramatic planes that bear up to more intense scrutiny, allowing the movie to evolve from one viewing to another.<br /><br />As one might be able to tell from this review, I am a huge fan of Nolan's, and have followed him from his beginning in, well, \\"Following,\\" as atypical a movie as one might find, perhaps with the exception of \\"Memento,\\" his next one, which is told in reverse order.  In and around the Batman tales, he has also directed \\"Insomnia,\\" \\"The Prestige\\" and \\"Inception,\\" which are no slouches as films, either.  (Incidentally, he and screenwriter David S. Goyer are the writers of the next \\"Superman\\" movie, due out next year.)<br /><br />I hope this year Nolan's \\"TDKR\\" is recognized by the Academy as one of the best movies of the year--it was an injustice that\\" The Dark Knight\\" was not.  Whether a really great film started from a more entertaining genre like a graphic novel should not count against it--\\"A History of Violence,\\" \\"V for Vendetta, \\" \\"Road to Perdition\\" and \\"American Splendor\\" are all landmark films deserving of serious consideration on their own.<br /><br />After all, Mark Twain and Charles Dickens were serialized in newpapers each week. B000002UA2|4.0|Like \\"More,\\" \\"Obscured by Clouds\\" is a soundtrack for a Barbet Schroeder film -- \\"The Valley,\\" in this case. Unlike \\"More,\\" the members of Pink Floyd clearly were ready for such a task, as this record is far superior.<br /><br />That's true even though -- again like \\"More\\" -- \\"Obscured by Clouds\\" features a number of instrumentals; of the 10 tracks here, only six have lyrics, with the rest being instrumentals. But the Floyd by this time had learned how to create atmosphere, so that even the instrumentals are evocative. Especially good along those lines: The title track, \\"Mudmen\\" and \\"Absolutely Curtains,\\" the latter of which does feature a vocal chant by the Mapuga tribespeople.<br /><br />But the songs with vocals are the most developed, and nearly every one of the four is very good. Exceptionally noteworthy is the haunting \\"Burning Bridges,\\" composed by guitarist David Gilmour and keyboardist Rick Wright; they share vocals on the song. Also deserving of merit is bassist Roger Waters' \\"Free Four.\\" Yes, lines like \\"the memories of a man in his old age / are the deeds of a man in his prime\\" can seem a bit quaint now, but this is one of Waters' early songs about losing his father -- a theme that would resurface on \\"The Wall\\" and really resonate on \\"The Final Cut.\\"<br /><br />I also like Gilmour's \\"Childhood's End,\\" inspired by the Arthur C. Clarke novel of the same name.<br /><br />Overall, this is a very fine effort from the Floyd. Certainly it doesn't hold together as well as the concept albums that would follow, but it's still a very worthy collection of songs.

B004ZN9UZO|4.0|More than a few critics were pretty hostile toward Pink Floyd's \\"Animals\\" when the album was released in 1977. Then again, the Floyd had set the bar so high on its previous two releases that it's not really surprising there would be a backlash. 1975's \\"Wish You Were Here\\" and 1973's \\"Dark Side of the Moon\\" still rank as two of the greatest British rock albums of all time, with their grand lyrical scope and excellent music.<br /><br />And those two albums SOUNDED so good -- they were like the rock 'n' roll equivalent of tropical jungles, incredibly lush and richly complex.<br /><br />\\"Animals\\" was still a sonic pleasure, but it was a step down, partly because Pink Floyd was setting up its own studio, and the equipment and recording spaces just were not up to the quality standards of Abbey Road, where they (and a band called the Beatles) had recorded previously.<br /><br />Furthermore, \\"Animals\\" had a much harder edge. Gone were the Dick Parry sax solos that had made \\"Dark Side\\" and \\"Wish You Were Here\\" sound so warm and appealing. In their place, guitarist David Gilmour was delving into a more aggressive style; his playing always had been quite good, but it never rocked as hard as it does here.<br /><br />Finally, there was the issue of songwriting. Of the five cuts -- the two parts of \\"Pigs on the Wing,\\" plus the longer \\"Dogs,\\" \\"Pigs (Three Different Ones)\\" and \\"Sheep\\" -- two were retreads. \\"Dogs,\\" previously called \\"You Gotta Be Crazy,\\" and \\"Sheep,\\" once known as \\"Raving and Drooling,\\" had not made the cut for \\"Wish You Were Here.\\" Bassist/vocalist/lyricist Roger Waters rewrote them, introducing the animal themes to go along with the \\"Pigs\\" songs and hey, presto! A new concept album was born, cast very much in the likness of George Orwell's \\"Animal Farm,\\" where the pigs are the intellectual but morally ambiguous overlords, the dogs are their \\"muscle,\\" and the sheep must follow blindly or be torn to pieces.<br /><br />This was Waters' scathing commentary on late-1970s English ennui and bitterness. Economic woes and a general sense of unease really were in the air -- and everyone knows that in bad times, people do tend to become a bit more animalistic.<br /><br />\\"Dogs\\" is my favorite track here. The lack of saxes aside, the 17-minute song features some absolutely blazing Gilmour guitar work. And the song boasts something a lot of fans (and critics) have missed over the years. Gilmour sings the first half of it ... and he sings in the second-person point of view, too (\\"you\\" this and \\"you\\" that). After the instrumental break, Waters takes over the vocals through the song's end, and Waters' contribution is in FIRST person (\\"I gotta admit...\\" and so on). The shift -- from Gilmour's more tuneful voice to Waters' more brittle one, from the slight distance provided by the second person to the incredibly withering personal feel of the first person -- is at once subtle, dramatic and effective.<br /><br />The other songs here are not as good. \\"Pigs on the Wing,\\" split into two parts on most formats, opens and closes the album. Musical historians and dinosaurs such as myself will remember that there was an 8-track-only version, which joined the two pieces with a solo by Snowy White, who played second guitar on some Floyd tours (Gilmour declined to do it himself, for some reason). \\"Pigs (Three Different Ones)\\" is pushed along by a swirling organ part from keyboardist Rick Wright, and it sounds positively dated after the synth sounds Wright had created for \\"Wish You Were Here.\\"<br /><br />\\"Sheep,\\" the album closer, was actually quite a powerful song, even though lyrically it displayed the same bitterness that marked the rest of \\"Animals.\\"<br /><br />Flaws or no, this album has long been a favorite of mine. My top two PF albums are \\"Dark Side\\" and \\"Wish,\\" but I would put \\"Animals\\" third -- ahead of \\"The Wall\\" and definitely better than \\"The Final Cut,\\" \\"A Momentary Lapse of Reason\\" and \\"The Division Bell.\\"<br /><br />But this is also the album on which serious cracks began to develop in Floydworld -- big divisions and bad feelings between band members. For one thing, previously prolific keyboardist Wright had no songs to contribute, which angered Waters, who felt he was shouldering more and more of the creative burden (Gilmour co-wrote, with Waters, the music for \\"Dogs\\"). Meanwhile, Gilmour and Waters continued their bickering over how the Floyd should sound, with Waters preferring a more spare, \\"drier\\" sound and Gilmour pushing for more reverb and gloss. By the time \\"The Wall\\" was finished, Waters would insist Wright be fired. Waters himself then left the band after \\"The Final Cut,\\" and Gilmour and drummer Nick Mason brought Wright back for the two subsequent studio albums.<br /><br />Not until 2005 would Gilmour, Mason and Wright reunite with Waters, and then it was for a one-off performance at the Live 8 concert in London. Wright died of cancer in 2008, apparently bringing the Floyd era to its end.<br /><br />\\"Animals\\" is a reminder of how Pink Floyd sounded at their most forceful and bellicose. It's a great record to revisit after all these years.bum on which serious cracks began to develop in Floydworld -- big divisions and bad feelings between band members. For one thing, previously prolific keyboardist Wright had no songs to contribute, which angered Waters, who felt he was shouldering more and more of the creative burden (Gilmour co-wrote, with Waters, the music for \\"Dogs\\"). Wright died of cancer in 2008, apparently bringing the Floyd era to its end.<br /><br />\\"Animals\\" is a reminder of how Pink Floyd sounded at their most forceful and bellicose. It's a great record to revisit after all these years.

B000SEIK2S|2.0|Given the large number of positive reviews, I was disappointed with the actual product.  As noted, the book introduces a universe in which humans exist in Darwinian competition with alien species and retired citizens are rejuvenated to fight in defense of humanity.  The book has three sections, each of which is disappointing in its own way.<br /><br />The book starts well; I bought it after reading the sample chapter.  The first section introduces the main character, a 75 year-old widower, and follows him through the process of induction into the Colonial Defense Forces and physical rejuvenation.  This section introduces a number of interesting ideas: the species in the universe are nearly all expansionist and compete violently for resources; the ideal colonists come from high birth-rate cultures or countries; old people make good soldiers; and Earth, while still the population center of humanity, is technologically inferior to its colonies.  Unfortunately, none of these interesting ideas are explored in any depth.  The idea of 'old' soldiers is the only topic that gets much discussion and even then in extremely superficial ways: jokes about having to pee often and the assumption that the first desire of rejuvenated geriatrics is to have lots of sex.<br /><br />The second section deals with training and fighting in the Colonial Defense Forces.  It is essentially very bad military sci-fi.  The drill instructor is stereotyped.  The military procedure details have mistakes (e.g. first platoons have numbers and companies have letters to designate them; later platoons have letters).  The main character advances in a silly, idealistic way: he luckily impresses the drill sergeant; he has the best ideas in a training competition; he saves the day in his first battle; he saves the day in another battle.  He gets promoted from corporal to lieutenant (as though this is a normal military reward) and receives no officer training.  Actual character development is essentially nil.<br /><br />The third section introduces a mystery concerning the 'Ghost Brigades,' and the main character's deceased wife.  I'll avoid giving away the details, but this section too disappointed.  Some (new) complex issues are explored a little here, but only briefly.  Things come together too conveniently.  Elements of the romance feel contrived rather than emotionally engaging.  The action is again very poor (grenades are 'tossed' at one moment and fired from rifles the next; a senior special forces officer forgets where she put her ammunition).<br /><br />The bottom line is that the book has little to commend it except for a breezy story line and a few interesting plot twists.  These kept me reading through the end, but ultimately I was sorry that I had wasted my time and money.

B004LUHQ1G|5.0|As long as I've been listening to music, the Foo Fighters have been one of my favorite bands.  I first came conscious of them with [[ASIN:B000QEIORG THE COLOUR AND THE SHAPE]], and I've bought all of their records within the first week of release since.  One of my favorite things about the band is that no two albums sound quite the same.  This time around, the band's is refined (or roughened) with the help of producer Butch Vig.  The alternative pop-rock that propelled [[ASIN:B000020617 THERE IS NOTHING LEFT TO LOSE]] was nowhere to be found in their next album.  The gloomy moods of [[ASIN:B00006JO4T ONE BY ONE]] weren't repeated in [[ASIN:B0009HLDFU IN YOUR HONOR.]]  In 2011's WASTING LIGHT, the band mostly sheds its acoustics for rougher edges -- the nuance and versatility of [[ASIN:B00138JAUY ECHOES, SILENCE, PATIENCE, & GRACE]] has been traded in for a brawling brand of rock-n-roll that was hinted in more recent albums.  One thing is for sure though: Dave Grohl & company are able to create tunes just as melodic and solid as anything they've ever done.<br /><br />Opening track \\"Bridge Burning\\" pulls no punches -- it begins the album on one of its heavier tracks.  This song and \\"White Limo\\" form some of the heaviest tracks the Foo Fighters have ever released.  The aggression isn't wasted: these songs are tumbling, rocking tracks.  The second track is WASTING LIGHT's lead single, \\"Rope.\\"  The song finds Grohl crooning over the sharp, rough verses before giving way to the melodic and powerful chorus.  One of my favorites off the album (though I recognize it's not the best) is \\"Arlandria,\\" a song about Grohl's hometown roots.  The song exemplifies one of my favorite things about the Foo Fighters: their albums are chock-full of great songs, even those that don't serve well as radio-singles (this song seemed to get the least amount of attention compared to others).  \\"These Days\\" begins a bit more mellow before diving into a brawling chorus -- Grohl's has a natural talent for pulling melodies out of songs, and this song is a great examples.  \\"I Should Have Known,\\" the albums penultimate track, is probably the most sonically interesting on the album.  Where most of WASTING LIGHT relegates itself to standard guitar-based arrangements, this song feels a little bluesy and a little more heart-felt than others.  The album closes with \\"Walk;\\" it's an optimistic song that stands out for it's happier attitude.  In an album full of aggression and angst, \\"Walk\\" ends the album on a good note.<br /><br />I was going to originally give this album 4 stars out of 5, because it is one of the Foo Fighters' albums that I revisit the least.  Putting things into perspective though, even though this album isn't the band's strongest, it's so much better than many albums that are released each year.  Additionally, it's incredibly consistent -- there's not a bad song in the entire bunch.<br /><br />When most bands lately seem to tamper down their rougher edges using Pro-Tools, synthesizers, or electronics, the Foo Fighters move in the opposite direction: WASTING LIGHT is the band's roughest, heaviest album to date.  Essential tracks to download: \\"Rope,\\" \\"White Limo,\\" \\"These Days,\\" and \\"Walk.\\"  If you have any interest in the Foo Fighters, be sure to check out this album.nt for pulling melodies out of songs, and this song is a great examples.  \\"I Should Have Known,\\" the albums penultimate track, is probably the most sonically interesting on the album. 0805095535|3.0|The cover copy describes this as an \\"unconventional memoir in which [Auster] writes about his mother's life and death.\\" Which is true, but only up to a point. There is one section that deals particularly with the life and death of Auster's mother, but it takes up only about 35 of 230 pages. That material, which also appeared in Granta 117, is easily the strongest in WINTER JOURNAL. Poignant, vivid, imbued simultaneously with his sense of his mother's individual tragedy and his awareness that on another level she must always remain a mystery to him, it's a model of the personal essay. Unfortunately, what surrounds it is (barring a charmingly sentimental description of his second marriage) formless, frequently dull recollections that neither capture Auster's visceral experience nor reveal anything about the human condition. It's all well-written on a basic level, full of long sentences that flow naturally and are never difficult to parse, but beyond that ease of reading there are few rewards.<br /><br />In Michael Chabon's WONDER BOYS, a character critiques the protagonist's still-incomplete gargantuan novel by suggesting that the inclusion of such details as \\"the genealogies of the horses\\" represents an inability to focus. WINTER JOURNAL is very short, but betrays a similar failure of focus. Nearly 60 pages are given over to a descriptive list of Auster's 21 permanent addresses over the years. There's also a catalog of scars and the stories behind them. Of course one appreciates the intimations of mortality and resulting reflections on the past that drove Auster to make these lists, but for readers lacking their own intimations and reflections, the resonance of this journal may be minimal. None of the homes and scars and snacks are described intensely enough for them to live on in the reader's mind as they do on Auster's, and so they are easily forgotten when another topic juts into frame. There is something to be said for an unconventional, unstructured approach to memoir, but there's also something to be said for a center of gravity, without which such a book becomes a weightless series of reminiscences. For admirers of Auster, especially those interested in his life, and for those who like him are inspired by middle age to consider the sheer vastness of a single life, WINTER JOURNAL may be worth an afternoon's perusal; for others, there is no particular reason to read it at all.memoir, but there's also something to be said for a center of gravity, without which such a book becomes a weightless series of reminiscences. B000MX7V5M|5.0|My preference is for Hitchcock's grittier suspense movies, but &quot;To Catch a Thief&quot; has so many things going for it, I can't give it less than 5 stars!<br /><br />It's the early 50's and in an introduction, we see the arms only of a black-gloved thief stealing jewelry. Then to a French Villa, where the housekeeper sees a newspaper heading: &quot;The Cat Prowls Again?&quot; The column continues, &quot;Is it true, or just a rumor - that John Robie, former cat burglar of Paris before the war, is once again on the prowl? Fashionable resorts on the Riviera are being regularly looted by a skillful jewel thief. Robie, once a hero of the French Resistance Army, was said to have reformed - however, the style of this new crime wave is certainly his.&quot;<br /><br />Robie, in his French sailor-like striped sweater with rakish red &amp; white scarf, is not amused when the police arrive in force to &quot;talk&quot; to him. He escapes (cleverly) and goes to the restaurant of another reformed thief/Resistance fighter, Bertani. Robie tells him that he is not behind the new robberies, but no body believes him: &quot;What I can't understand is how this thief can imitate me so perfectly. It has to be someone who knew every detail of my technique.... I've got to catch this imitator myself.&quot;<br /><br />And that's what he does, aided by Mr. H.H. Hughson, an insurance agent who is very nervous indeed. Hughson manages an introduction to a likely future burglary victim, the redoubtable Mrs. Stevens and her cool calm and exceedingly beautiful daughter, Frances.<br /><br />There are so many fabulous scenes between Grace Kelly and Cary Grant that it needs a book to describe them. And Grant has his humor on in several more scenes, such as when he's trying to escape through a flower market and a diminutive gray-haired flower seller stops him.<br /><br />These are the extras on the one-disc DVD &quot;Special Collector's Edition&quot;:<br /><br />1. Commentary by Peter Bogdanovich and Laurent Bouzereau. Bogdanovich knew both Hitchcock and Grant, and Bouzereau co-wrote a book about Hitchcock with the director's daughter, Pat Hitchcock. &quot;To Catch a Thief&quot; was filmed right after the very successful &quot;Rear Window&quot;, and Bogdanovich said that the later movie has more of a vacation quality to it; Hitchcock could be more relaxed. In his own words, Hitchcock could rely on the charm of two leading actors &quot;who were beyond charming&quot;.<br /><br />Grant was 50 years old when he filmed &quot;To Catch a Thief&quot;, and he looks great. Bogdanovich and Bouzereau agree that he was in great shape, even though he never exercised. Or, rather, Bogdanovich tells us, Grant once told him that &quot;he never exercised, he just made love.&quot;<br /><br />Grace Kelly fell in love with Prince Rainier right after &quot;To Catch a Thief&quot;, so it's her last movie.<br /><br />2. &quot;Writing and Casting 'To Catch a Thief' &quot; a 2002 short with commentary by Pat Hitchcock (his daughter), Mary Stone (Hitchcock's granddaughter) and Steven deRosa (author, &quot;Writing with Hitchcock&quot;), accompanied by movie shots and stills as well as personal movies and photos.  One of the things they touch on is how Hitchcock got the innuendo-laden dialogue and shots between Grant and Kelley past the PCA (Production Code Association).<br /><br />3. &quot;The Making of 'To Catch a Thief' &quot;. The extras # 2, 3 and 4 were pretty much all filmed together, and then they divided them into three extras. Nothing wrong with it, but they could have put it into one long featurette.  Commentators in this short are Mary Stone, Doc Erickson (production manager), Steven deRosa, Pat Hitchcock and Sylvette Baudrot (continuity in France). Baudrot remembers that Grant showed up for filming the beginning in a dress shirt with buttonholes on the collar. The film was supposed to take place right after the war, in '48-'49, and Baudrot told Hitchcock that the shirt-style was more modern and American, at that. And that's why John Robie is wearing a sweater with a scarf tied around his neck!<br /><br />I also got a kick out of one of Mary Stone's reminisces. Her grandfather liked happy endings, she says, but he liked to have a last little twist at the end. In this case, the very last scene has Grant and Kelly embracing on his villa's porch and Kelly murmers, &quot;So this is where you live. Oh, Mother will love it up here.&quot;!!<br /><br />4. &quot;Alfred Hitchcock and 'To Catch a Thief': An Appreciation &quot; The same people as #2 &amp; 3. Still interesting tidbits, just not sure why they divided it up like this.<br /><br />5. &quot;Edith Head - The Paramount Years&quot;.  This documentary was filmed in 2002 and has also been an extra on a DVD release of &quot;Sunset Blvd.&quot;. I hadn't seen this documentary before, however, and I really enjoyed it. Head's career spanned 60 years, 40 of them with Paramount. Commentors include David Chierichetti (Edith Head biographer), Tzetzi Ganev (head of Custom-Made Dept for Western Costume at Paramount), Bob Mackie (fashion designer) and Rosemary Clooney (actor, who knew Head from the movie &quot;White Christmas&quot;).<br /><br />Head got her first big break when the lead costume designer didn't get along with mega-star Clara Bow. He gave Bow's assignments to Head, who learned a big lesson from that which she carried throughout her whole career: Get along with everybody.<br /><br />Head had told people that Hitchcock was her favorite director, and &quot;To Catch a Thief&quot; was &quot;her all time favorite film&quot;.<br /><br />6. Trailer<br /><br />Lovely movie. I watch it for the humor - which can be tongue-in-cheek. Such as when Robie escapes by boarding a bus and ends up in the back seat. Between a woman with a bird in a cage and ... Alfred Hitchcock!<br /><br />Happy Readers more modern and American, at that. B008JMKN4Y|5.0|Abby is a student at Eastern University. She's left her life behind in Kansas, and taken her friend America along with her. She's hoping to escape who she was, hoping to never be remembered for the life she's leaving behind. America has promised to be her conscience, and together at Eastern, they have each other's backs.<br /><br />One night, while attending an inner Circle fight, Abby comes face to face with everything she's trying to leave behind. Travis Maddox is the playboy of Eastern University, but for Abby, whom he calls Pigeon, he's willing to leave his playboy life behind. But Abby is resistant to his charms. They have both met their match and whether together or apart, they are a disaster waiting to happen.<br /><br />My Review<br /><br />*My goal with this review is to not give any spoilers! You just have to read this book for yourself!!<br /><br />It's completely a cliche, but....I COULDN'T PUT THIS BOOK DOWN!! There are so many things that I loved about this book, I just am not sure where to start....so I'll just dive right in!<br />The first thing I LOVED about this book was Abby. Innocent, independent, beautiful, fun loving Abby. I loved this character from the beginning! She's moved to Eastern to escape a father who blames her for his downturn in life, and a mother who can't stay away from the bottle. Ready to start a life that's no where near what she left, she walks right into that same life when she comes face to face with bad boy Travis \\"Mad Dog\\" Maddox. She's completely resistant to his charms and has no intentions of being the next girl that he doesn't call back.<br />The next first thing I LOVED about this book is Travis. You just can't love Abby without also loving Travis. Travis is the buzz cut, tattooed, muscled-up playboy of Eastern University. There's practically a different girl every night on his \\"couch.\\" Travis isn't just rough and tough, never losing a fight in the \\"Circle\\" at Eastern, but he's also a frat brother, the man about campus, and a brainiac. When he comes face to face with innocent, cardigan wearing Abby at one of his fights, he has met his match. Abby isn't the only one trying to escape a past. Travis was raised by his dad and four older brothers, with little memory of his mother, who passed away when he was very young. Travis has built a wall, and Abby may be just the only person who will ever be allowed to knock it down.<br />The next first thing I LOVED about this book is Abby and Travis's story. Which is the entire book!! What a roller-coaster ride!<br />Some may think that Abby's weak and stupid, or even cruel and heartless, but at the age of 19, she's lived a life that most never do! It's a life she never wants to be known for, or to ever live again. That's the problem with Travis. He is a walking memory of everything that she's left behind. But it's more than that. Abby doesn't want to ever be blamed for being the person behind someone's downfall ever again.<br />Some may think that Travis is an arrogant bully who can't keep his pants on. Which is pretty accurate. But that all changes with Abby. There isn't much Travis wouldn't do for Abby, which is his downfall. But when Travis let's that wall down, it's a forever event. This is the Travis that I love most. I love the Travis that will lay his life down for his girl, even when she isn't his girl. I love the Travis that melts at her feet, and I love the Travis that he is with her. I don't love the Travis the comes out during the rough times.<br />What I loved most about Beautiful Disaster is everything!<br />This book isn't for the faint of heart. It's got a lot of language, sex, fighting, blood and gore. But it's got a heart and soul. This story breathes it's own life. It is a Beautiful Disaster.<br /><br />FTC required disclaimer: I received this book from the publisher. I did not receive monetary compensation for this review.

What that means for those of you who have never watched movies on VHS tape is that the screen is formatted for a squarish cathode ray television with a 4:3 aspect ratio, instead of the standard 16:9 ratio for widescreen HDTVs. So when you watch this, you're going to get the black bars on both sides of the screen. Yes, at first it is \\"widescreen\\" for a few seconds, but then it changes for the rest of the movie. In my opinion, this doesn't hurt the presentation.<br /><br />The movie itself is a victim of the storytelling of the late 70s and obvious budgetary constraints. That's not to say that this is a bad film, but it doesn't really hold up today, even against your typical B-movie actioner. Some of the acting is way over the top to the point of being ridiculous. I'm thinking of the barechested police chief in the black leather pants and black silk scarf smoking a cigar and watering his plants and yelling at Max down the stairs while still holding his orange watering can. But I guess in Australia in the  late 70s, this was pretty normal. I will say that the climax of the movie shows admirable restraint and I thought it was well-executed. The real draws here are Mel Gibson and the fact that this is the movie that sets the stage for The Road Warrior. That the Road Warrior came out of this movie is amazing. The quality of filmmaking rose proportional to the budget and what comes out of it is nothing less than a masterpiece. This movie, at the very least, has really awesome box art.

The borders on this version are a light grey.<br /><br />I was first drawn to Thoth and Crowley six years ago, at the very start of my Tarot and spiritual journey. I remember there being only two choices in my mind when I placed the order for my first Tarot deck: RWS or Thoth. I went with the Original RWS, and boy do I regret it! I made the choice due to the vast majority of material being used to learn Tarot was based on the RWS, and the common controversy over the Thoth (and what I'm now beginning to read into: Crowley himself). I'm now wondering where I would be if I had chosen the Thoth instead of or in addition to the Original RWS...<br />Since then I've had Thoth and Crowley in the back of my mind for years now!<br /><br />The deck itself is fantastic! I do not see what others claim is so scary about this deck. It is intense, it is detailed, it is bold, and it is beautiful! But scary? No! I simply do not see it. It is not morbid or dark. While I may be a little darker than the average person, I am usually very perceptive. This, however, I simply cannot wrap my head around.<br /><br />I was concerned with the common issue of the Thoth lacking scenic pips. I do not know if I was misinformed or misinterpreted what others have said in regards to the Minors, but for whatever reason I was under the impression they would be minimalistic. While they may not display a full scene of imagry, the pips are very talkative! The expressive style of Harris' rendering of them, in conjunction with the keywords make these Minors VERY readable! Add in astrological correspondences and Quabala, and BAM! -In your face meanings.<br /><br />I can see myself making the purchase of the Large Thoth deck for study and futher clairification of Harris' artwork. -Something I never thought necessary with the RWS! Those are so basic and bland in comparison to Harris's depiction. The imagry itself, in conjunction with Crowley's mad genius enlightenment make this deck stand far above RWS in my mind. Yes, Crowley was a stickler with Harris' artwork, but I have read that she pressured him to create the deck; she paid him money to tudor her in the ways of magick. But, as such, every symbol, every colour, every \\"backdrop\\" has meaning! I've read that The Book of Thoth and Thoth Tarot are Crowley's magnum opus, the final culmination of 45 years of magickal/esoteric/spiritual study. That in itself makes this deck far more approachable to me. I cannot wait for the Thoth to \\"meet me\\" at whatever level, and I cannot wait for it to grow and adapt to my knowledge base!<br /><br />Finally, I must add that the \\"official\\" Magus card is my favourite! The others are nice, too, but this one just \\"fits\\" with everything I know about the card. Perhaps it is not important to some, but the fact that I had/have a choice and chose the original speaks volumes to me. I am very individualistic, and I love personalization; but in this case I felt it was not necessary. Crowley's final decison resonated with me, and I'm thrilled!<br /><br />As far as it not being a \\"beginner's deck\\" - If you want it, go for it! I certainly wish I did. This deck has opened up so many new windows and doors of study for me and I'm loving every minute of it!<br /><br />***Do yourself a favour and buy DuQuette's 'Understanding Aleister Crowley's Thoth Tarot'. Then, or in conjunction with, buy/read Crowley's own 'Book of Thoth'.***<br /><br />**Then look into: 'The Thoth Companion: The Key to the True Symbolic Meaning of the Thoth Tarot' by Snuffin seems pretty good, as do 'The Crowley Tarot: The Handbook to the Cards' (more in-depth) & 'Keywords for the Crowley Tarot' (more concise & better for purely divination purposes) both by Banzhaf.**<br /><br />*Do NOT buy: 'Tarot: Mirror of the Soul: Handbook for the Aleister Crowley Tarot' by Ziegler OR 'The Tarot Handbook: Practical Applications of Ancient Visual Symbols' by Arrien. B006DDO1D0|5.0|This book is the result of a vivid imagination, a mountain of research, and a lot of heart. Stephen King's own life experience figures in as well (you will know when you hear that particular section). The nuance and depth of the story is stunning. King pulls no punches in terms of vivid portrayals of gore and mayhem (no surprise) but also shows a touching, poetic heart and tenderness in a beautiful, fully realized love story within the broader tale of Jake Epping's adventures into the world of the 1950's and 60's. Leitmotifs abound. I can understand why other reviewers have said they listen again. I have found that I often \\"rewind\\" parts of the book, one of the more appealing aspects of listening to a recorded book. The plot is historically based with certain \\"fudging\\" as King himself admits, but who cares. We are engrossed and fully involved. By the telling, you really have no idea how things are going to turn out! Right up to the crucial moment itself on the date of the title we don't know what will happen, and the coda to the main plot action is extremely satisfying.<br /><br />The reader of the audio book, Craig Wasson, not an 'A' list name - although a familiar one - shows his considerable chops as dialect and accent specialist here, as well as an emotionally involved and sympathetic performer. His voice characterizations are consistent and committed, many voices for the many characters, and each distinct in its own way. Of course, there are expected stereotypes, many of them funny and endearing. King's language is often visceral and blunt, and Wasson's delivery is terrific. This must have been a grueling process, reading and recording all this. The result is very much worth it. I started listening on a long car trip and was immediately engrossed in the unfolding of the tale, which has a circularity and interconnectivity to it that is remarkable. The return trip, and subsequent car and air travel provided more opportunities to return to the story. I was eager to, and knowing the next chapter was waiting was a great pleasure.<br /><br />I love audio books, and this masterful riff on history is one of the very best. Highest recommendation.

It is apparently a quick way to set up some energy saving functions, and when it is on,  Wake On LAN can not be enabled.  If disable EuP, you can enable WOL.  Would be nice if it were a bit clearer what this particular function is for.  The text for it says \\"Enable EuP function\\".  Thanks.<br /><br />2) This particular CPU (the I5 2400) does not support hyperthreads.  This is not Shuttle's fault; I should have checked the Intel web site more carefully.  I looked to be sure it supported multiple cores and virtualization accelerations, but did not even notice the hyperthreads.  Not a big deal for me, just a surprise.<br /><br />Oct 10, 2012:<br /><br />One thing that I never mentioned, and pretty small:  The 'suspend' function works well, but there is no indication from the outside whether the system is off or in suspend.  Usually systems flash the power light when itis suspended.  I thought there might be a BIOS setting, but I could not find one.

Now....down the road if you want to keep going, you're going to need some other rule books to expand your adventures, but this is a GREAT starter set. In this case, uncontrollable grief along with incessant sobbing.  Once that period ends the ability to smell goes as well.<br /><br />Eva Green plays Susan, an epidemiologist who is working on the identification process.  Her apartment overlooks the back door of an upscale restaurant where Michael (Ewan McGregor) is the chef.  Both are coming off badly ended relationships, but of course manage to hook-up.  What do you expect, they're both great looking.  While their relationship becomes hotter, so does the virus.  One by one, new psychological symptoms arrive with increasingly devastating effects. For example, even without a sense of taste, I doubt I'd want to be ingesting soap and shaving cream just for a few bathtub chuckles.<br /><br />Although the movie has sadness about it and a bleak forecast, Susan and Michael's love story is both erotic and hopeful. The transfer has a 2.34:1 aspect ratio nearly the same as the original print and is of course 1080p.  While the picture isn't reference quality it is likely close to the original.  Several scenes appear to be stock newsreel footage in standard definition.  I believe this was shot with digital cameras.  Some scenes look a little dark but overall not a big deal.  The audio is DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1.  It is very good.  There are no booming noises so expect a pretty quiet film.  So quiet in fact, I had to play it at a much higher level than I usually do. 0375846735|1.0|I had the displeasure of reading this book twice. Once at the tender age of 9, then at 20. Why did I read a book I did not like twice, you may ask? Because I LOVED The Golden Compass and The Subtle Knife. As I grew, I got to thinking maybe I had been unfair with the third one; maybe I was too young to understand it, maybe I hadn't paid attention, whatever the case could be. So I gave it a second chance and holy damn was it painful.<br /><br />The book is full of tired clich&eacute;s, plot devices, deus ex machina, corny dialogue, corny writing in general, terrible characterisation, horrid pacing, and the list goes on. To go in a little bit more detail:<br /><br />Firstly, one the things that most annoyed me was how Pullman utterly DESTROYED his formerly brilliant characters. Whatever happened to the strong-minded, willful, intelligent Lyra? In this book, she feels like a lame vehicle for the author to make a very poorly thought out metaphor, and a terribly written love interest. She just falls into being this one-dimensional, unintelligent, cowardly little girl! You get this tired impression that inside, she's just going \\"oh I hope Will can protect me, I'm so scared!\\", which is SO unlike the previous two books it makes my blood boil. I also felt the same for Mrs. Coulter; whatever happened to her? Where's the devious, machinating woman from the Golden Compass? I mean goddamnit.<br /><br />The author also has the balls to introduce something called an Intention Device, or some such, that can be used for LITERALLY ANYTHING but is instead meant TO BRING A CHARACTER FROM POINT A TO POINT B AND THEN PROMPTLY BREAK because he couldn't be assed to write himself out of his predictament. This is the most glaring deus ex machina in the story, but unfortunately not the only one. See the female angel that is used as AN EXPOSITION TOOL.<br /><br />The part with those godawful mulefa was so atrociously bad it makes me angry all over again just to remember it. It was boring. It was corny. It was lazy. It was so unlike the previous two books that, I swear to God, I honestly thought it had been written by a different person. It also felt like an incredibly long part, but then again any length of text with those things is too much. They are uninteresting, ridiculous, cheesy, and once again used to make some horribly contrived analogies to get the author's point across.<br /><br />The resolution to the Dust problem just SCREAMS of laziness. The love story was horrible, forced, contrived and painful to read (and NOT in the sense that it was actually \\"heart-wrenching\\", as some reviewers said). The villains were a one-note bore, ridiculous and one-dimensional, especially the priest who was in it for the evulz. I'm not even kidding, he meets some giant birds and they just know each other to be evil (the author wrote this into the book, I'm not even joking), and then the bird things promptly procceed to defecate on the mulefa houses while the priest takes a hike on one of them. You know what this subplot amounts to? Nothing. So do many others.<br /><br />You know what's actually heart wrenching? (spoilers for A Song of Ice and Fire, maybe, so be warned): \\"Jaime Lannister sends his regards\\". That is powerful, because you cared. This nauseating romance between Will and Lyra made me think(SPOILERS) \\"yes, please, separate these two, the world couldn't handle this much awkward and forced sappiness without self destructing out of shame\\".<br /><br />The writing itself just felt both forced and lazy. I distinctively remember one passage in which one of the two kids says that their atoms will meet each other when they die or some such. ARE YOU FOR REAL, PULLMAN? I mean seriously, if you're going with anti-theism, that's great, but own up to it and go all the way; don't force me to swallow this kind of crap.<br /><br />Finally, as for the religious content itself, criticism of anything is fine as long as it's done intelligently and with good arguments. This was the case in the first two books (especially The Golden Compass); in fact, I'd argue Book 1 is a great example of criticism done right. However, this feels like reading Ayn Rand, I kid you not. Plot and characters were cast aside in favor of making some horribly written and poorly thought out metaphors, there is forced exposition, there are strawmen everywhere... the only thing that was lacking was a bona fide author fillibuster, if I recall correctly, but I wouldn't be surprised if on a second reread I could find this book's \\"This is John Galt speaking.\\"<br /><br />So, to describe this book in one word: lazy. The plot was done lazily; so were the characterisation, the theological arguments, the pacing, the dialogue... it's a freaking mess, and it only gets worse as the book goes on.<br /><br />My recommendation is read it, because you should always see things for yourself. Hell, you might be one of the many who love this book, for some reason. However, see if you can read it for free first, then buy it if you like it. This is not worth the paper it's printed on, IMO.<br /><br />One final note: I don't mean to offend anybody who liked it, taste is taste. These are only my opinions, and although they are harsh, it's only because I consider this to be the worst book I've ever read given the series it's in. If it were a stand alone book, I'd give it 3 stars, but given that it takes an amazing story and rips it in a million pieces, it felt like betrayal. It left a bitter taste in my mouth. I felt offended reading it, like Pullman was saying \\"Yeah, see this cool story? Watch me butcher it while it screams in agony\\".<br /><br />Well, that's it, hope there are some kindred spirits out there to balance out the negative votes I'll get, hehe.s the case in the first two books (especially The Golden Compass); in fact, I'd argue Book 1 is a great example of criticism done right. B003Y5H574|4.0|CRAZY, STUPID, LOVE is a dynamic and heartfelt comedy--a comedy with real laugh-out-loud moments and scenes where the viewers' heads are nodding because we've all felt the way the characters on screen are feeling.  It's warm, readily identifiable, poignant, substantially charming, and for the most part, totally believable.  It holds together and flows remarkably well--with an hysterical scene when four men go rogue--until it unravels during a surreal and bizarre junior high graduation.  And the air comes out of the balloon with a gaseous hiss, an ending this outstanding comedy doesn't deserve.<br /><br />Emily and Cal (Julianne Moore and Steve Carell) have been married over twenty years and look and act the part--until Emily announces she wants a divorce, as she's had an affair with a coworker (Kevin Bacon).  Stunned and confused, Cal is suddenly a middle-aged single man, practically wearing the 'D' word on his brow as he attends the local watering hole on a nightly basis, catching the attention of Jacob (Ryan Gosling), a suave babe magnet who effortlessly picks up any woman he wants, with no strings attached.  Jacob becomes Cal's wing man, teaching him how to dress, how to act, and particularly how to interact.  Although not nearly as successful as Jacob, Cal does periodically \\"score\\", particularly with perky Kate (Marisa Tomei), who, unbeknownst to both of them, is the teacher of Cal's 13-year-old son Robbie (Jonah Bobo).  Yet despite his conquests Cal feels strangely empty, and still drawn to Emily.  Meanwhile Robbie has a crush on Jessica (Analeigh Tipton), the babysitter, and if that's not complicated enough Jessica has a crush on Cal.  And the icing on this neopolitan cake is when ladies' man Jacob falls head over heels for Emily and Cal's elder daughter Hannah (Emma Stone); and the stage is set for some crazy, stupid comedy.<br /><br />Character actor John Caroll Lynch rounds out the main players as Jessica's father, with Lynch, Bacon, Gosling, and Carell in a tag team foursome that had me donkey braying with glee.  It's all grand fun until Robbie, his eighth grade class valedictorian, gives a grim, existential speech at junior high graduation, with Cal interrupting to wax philosophical on true love, with me squirming uncomfortably, wondering if screenwriter Dan Fogelman had suddenly lapsed into a Jack Daniels funk.  The only persons who might enjoy this scene are therapists, and based on the bizarre utterings there's plenty of work to be done here.  Thus CRAZY, STUPID, LOVE is good, often very good, in spite of an almost fatal ending.  I suggest you have some Jack yourself before tackling it.<br />--D. B007X4ECLY|3.0|The prison takeover film has been a Hollywood staple for decades and Lockout runs with the formula with the only real snippet of originality being that the prison is set in space.  It is the year 2079, and CIA agent Marion Snow (Pearce) is framed for the murder of another agent who was investigating stolen government secrets.  Snow is sentenced to 30 years in prison at MS One, a maximum security prison set on a floating space satellite where prisoners are kept in a cryogenic stasis.<br /><br />Before his sentence can be carried out, however, Snow is given a chance at a reprieve.  The prisoners have broken out aboard MS One and have taken several people hostage, including Emilie, the daughter of the President, who was on the satellite on a humanitarian mission investigating the well-being of the prisoners.  Snow is initially reluctant to take on the mission until he learns that Mace, the man he gave the briefcase containing information that could clear him, is aboard the prison as well.  More determined to find the case rather than rescue Emilie, Snow sneaks onto the vessel to find it overrun with the most dangerous prisoners on the planet, led by Alex, and his psychotic kill-crazy brother Hydell.<br /><br />The entire pretext for the prison breakout is incredibly flimsy...First the daughter of the President going into such a dangerous location with little more than a couple of Secret Service agents.  Then the whole idea of the warden unfreezing perhaps the most mentally unhinged prisoner out of some 500 total is ludicrous.  It would be like someone going to Corcoran Prison in California to check on prisoners and the warden wheels out Charlie Manson.  And of course one of the Secret Service agents are dumb enough to sneak a gun into the interview room even though they were supposed to check all weapons.  Now remember, this is supposed to be 67 years in the future...So what, they stopped using metal detectors and Backscatter X-ray machines in 2079 and now just take guys words that they are unarmed?  Right...<br /><br />I've been a fan of Guy Pearce ever since Ravenous and have always enjoyed his performances, but he seems to be trying to channel a combination of Snake Plisken from Escape from New York and John McClane from Die Hard with his performance.  He forces one bad one-liner after another and ends up being more of a parody of action heroes than an actual action hero.  Thankfully Pearce is bailed out by a solid supporting cast including Maggie Grace as Emilie, and Peter Stromare as Langral, the head of the Secret Service.<br /><br />The action in Lockout is wildly inconsistent.  On one hand the prison has an automated defense system of Death Star like gun turrets that are able to repel the aircraft of security forces that Langral sends in to re-take the prison.  On the other hand, Snow is able to escape at least a half dozen times from prisoners just by running through a door and closing it behind him.  Why is it he seems to be able to operate all the doors and the prisoners can't?  Lockout is a futuristic film but it didn't have the budget ($20 million) to truly pull off a futuristic look.  This unrated edition amps up the violence a little bit but this could still really pass for a PG-13 film.<br /><br />Ultimately, Lockout is a middle of the road actioner that provides a mild diversion but is completely forgettable.ys words that they are unarmed? Wow that even sounds like the plot of a video game.<br /><br />The main problem with Wrath of the Titans is that it doesn't have a captivating villain to carry the action. Apparently this is a VERY common problem with this device, so it would be my advice to anyone considering it that they only be using it with their computer.
